
<h1>Sampling Methodology Summary</h1>
<h3>Multi-Sectoral Needs Assessment — North-West, North-East and North-Central Nigeria, 2026</h3>
<p>This note describes the sampling design used to select households for interview across the host and internally displaced (IDP) populations covered by this assessment. It is written as a methodology reference, not an implementation log — it does not describe the software, data-processing steps, or tools used to build the sample, only the statistical design itself and the reasoning behind it. Sections marked <strong>Technical detail</strong> throughout are intended to eventually move into a technical annex; everything else is written for a general, non-technical reader. It is intended as a first, comprehensive draft to be trimmed and adapted into whatever format is needed for the final Terms of Reference or technical annex.</p>
<hr>
<h2>1. Sampling frame and stratification</h2>
<p>The assessment covers host and internally displaced (IDP) populations across three regions of northern Nigeria — North-West, North-East and North-Central — spanning fourteen states. Host and IDP populations are treated as two entirely separate sampling frames throughout the design, reflecting both the different population data available for each and, as described in Section 3, a different field procedure for each.</p>
<p>The sampling universe was built from a regular hexagonal grid covering the assessment area, rather than directly from administrative or settlement boundaries. A regular grid gives every part of the landscape an equal opportunity to be represented in the frame and avoids the irregular shapes and highly uneven sizes of using Local Government Area (LGA) or ward polygons as the base sampling unit. Two independent population layers were then attributed to this grid, one per population group:</p>
<ul>
<li><strong>Host population</strong> — drawn from a gridded population estimate for Nigeria, giving an estimated number of people and households in each grid cell.</li>
<li><strong>IDP population</strong> — drawn from site-level location data collected under the International Organization for Migration's Displacement Tracking Matrix (DTM), which records IDP and returnee sites together with their GPS coordinates and an estimated number of households and individuals present at each. Each recorded site's population was attributed to whichever grid cell contains it.</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>Technical detail.</strong> The host population layer is WorldPop's constrained gridded population estimate for Nigeria (100m resolution, 2026 projection, release R2025A). A grid cell — for either population layer — is retained in the sampling frame only if its estimated household count exceeds 5 (equivalent to roughly 30 people, using an assumed average household size of 6). Cells at or below this threshold are treated as having no meaningful population and are dropped before stratification; the same threshold is applied identically to the host and IDP layers.</p>
<p>Grid cells with no meaningful population under a given layer were excluded from the corresponding frame. As a result, a host cluster can only ever be drawn from a cell known to have a resident host population, and an IDP cluster can only ever be drawn from a cell containing at least one recorded IDP or returnee site — there is no possibility of a cluster being assigned to an empty or unpopulated location by construction.</p>
<p>Both frames were then stratified by Admin-2 (Local Government Area) and population type together, producing up to two strata per Admin-2 — one host, one IDP, wherever both populations are present. Each stratum was treated as an independent sampling domain with its own required sample size, its own selection of clusters, and its own design weight. Stratifying at Admin-2 level, the level the assessment reports findings against, ensures the sample is spread proportionately across administrative units rather than concentrating in a small number of high-population districts.</p>
<p>Before either population layer was gridded, a further exclusion was applied to define the accessible area available for sampling in the first place: an area along Nigeria's international borders, and a set of areas separately assessed as inaccessible, were both removed from the sampling universe entirely (see Section 4 for the exact rule).</p>
<p>The map below shows the full assessment area: host population density, IOM DTM IDP sites (scaled by population), and the areas excluded from the sampling universe entirely.</p>
<p>*[Map: Assessment area — host population density (gradient), IOM DTM IDP sites (scaled by population), and areas excluded from the sampling universe (border buffer + FACT-assessed inaccessible admin-3 areas). See output/methodology_map_overview_v2.png.]*</p>
<hr>
<h2>2. Stage 1: primary sampling unit selection</h2>
<p>Within each stratum, the number of clusters required was calculated using a standard two-stage cluster-survey sample size formula, combining:</p>
<ul>
<li>a 90% confidence level,</li>
<li>a conservative assumption of a 50% expected proportion for the indicators being measured (the assumption that maximises the required sample size, and so a safe default where the true proportion is not known in advance),</li>
<li>a 10% margin of error,</li>
<li>a design effect reflecting the extra imprecision introduced by clustering households together rather than sampling them fully at random, based on an assumed intra-cluster correlation coefficient and a cluster size of six households per cluster,</li>
<li>a finite-population correction using each stratum's estimated household population, and</li>
<li>a 10% buffer added to the resulting figure to allow for attrition and cluster-level non-response, before rounding up to a whole number of six-household clusters.</li>
</ul>
<p>Strata with a very small total household population — below the equivalent of six clusters' worth of households — were treated as <strong>certainty strata</strong>: every eligible grid cell in that stratum was included in the sample directly, rather than a subset being drawn, since the underlying population is too small to sample from meaningfully. All other strata used <strong>probability-proportional-to-size (PPS)</strong> sampling, in which the chance of a given grid cell being selected as a cluster is proportional to its estimated household population — the same measure of size used to build the frame in the first place (the gridded population estimate for host strata; the DTM-reported household count for IDP strata). A more densely populated location is therefore proportionately more likely to be selected than a sparsely populated one, which keeps the achieved sample self-weighting toward where most of the population actually lives, while still giving every eligible location in the stratum a non-zero chance of selection.</p>
<p>PPS selection was implemented as a systematic sample with a random starting point: grid cells within a stratum were placed in a fixed order, a random starting point was chosen along the cumulative population total for that stratum, and clusters were then selected at fixed population intervals along that ordering. Because the interval is fixed while the population of individual grid cells varies, a single very large or densely populated cell can occasionally be selected more than once within the same stratum. Where this happened, the repeated selections were combined into a single cluster carrying a proportionally larger household quota, rather than being treated as two separate cluster visits to the same physical location.</p>
<p>A fixed random seed was used for this Stage 1 selection, so that, given the same input population data, the exact set of selected clusters can be reproduced — an important property for an assessment that may need to demonstrate its sample was drawn consistently and without manual interference.</p>
<hr>
<h2>3. Stage 2: household selection procedure</h2>
<p>Stage 2 — selecting which specific households within a chosen cluster are actually approached for interview — was designed and implemented differently for host and IDP clusters. This reflects a genuine, deliberate difference in what data is available and reliable for identifying individual households in each context, and is the single most important operational distinction for field teams to understand (see the companion field guide for how this translates into instructions for enumerators).</p>
<h3>Host clusters: building-footprint household selection</h3>
<p>For host clusters, individual building footprints identified from high-resolution satellite imagery were used as a proxy for household locations within the selected grid cell. Footprints were filtered to a minimum detection-confidence threshold and to a plausible residential size range, which excludes both very small non-residential fragments — such as outbuildings, latrines, or storage structures frequently mapped as separate footprints within a single household compound — and unusually large structures unlikely to house a single household. From the resulting set of eligible building locations within a cluster, six households were selected at random, without replacement, as the primary sample, with a further set of up to six additional households drawn and ranked in order as a reserve list. Field teams use this reserve list strictly in rank order whenever a primary household cannot be reached or declines to participate.</p>
<p><strong>Technical detail.</strong> A building footprint is eligible if its detection confidence score is at least 0.75 and its footprint area is between 12m² and 1,000m². The lower bound excludes small non-residential fragments (outbuildings, latrines, storage structures); the upper bound excludes very large structures — warehouses, markets, institutional buildings — unlikely to be a single household's residence.</p>
<p>The example below shows a real selected host cluster: the hexagon boundary, every eligible building footprint identified within it, and the six primary and six reserve households drawn from that pool.</p>
<p>*[Map: Example host cluster — hexagon boundary, every eligible building footprint within it, and the six primary and six reserve households drawn from that pool. See output/methodology_map_host_v2.png.]*</p>
<p>*[Map: Close-up of the selected host households against nearby buildings. See output/methodology_map_host_closeup.png.]*</p>
<p>A small number of selected grid cells contained no building footprint meeting the eligibility criteria at all — typically very sparsely built-up or marginal locations. Rather than leaving these clusters empty, each was reallocated to a different, previously unselected grid cell within the same stratum, drawn using the identical population-proportional selection method as the original Stage 1 draw. This preserves the statistical integrity of the design — the replacement cluster's chance of selection is calculated exactly as any other cluster's would be — while ensuring every cluster in the final sample corresponds to a real, visitable location with an actual population of eligible households to draw from.</p>
<h3>IDP clusters: site-based household selection</h3>
<p>The original design intended to use the same building-footprint method for IDP clusters. This was found, during the course of building the sample, not to work well for IDP locations specifically, for two related reasons.</p>
<p>First, a practical constraint: IDP locations exist in the sampling frame only where they have actually been recorded as populated in the underlying site-level data. Unlike host areas, there is no large surrounding pool of unselected grid cells to draw on if a given site's satellite-derived building footprints prove unreliable or unavailable — checking this directly found that, in the large majority of affected districts, every eligible IDP location had already been drawn into the sample, leaving no possibility of a like-for-like substitute of the kind used for host clusters.</p>
<p>Second, a data-quality consideration: satellite-derived building footprints are a weaker proxy for individual households in many IDP and informal-settlement contexts than in typical host neighbourhoods, since shelter arrangements in such settings can be denser, more temporary, or simply not correspond one-to-one with distinct visible structures from overhead imagery.</p>
<p>For these reasons, the household selection method for IDP clusters uses the site's own recorded GPS location directly, rather than satellite-derived building footprints. Every selected IDP cluster corresponds to a site recorded in the DTM dataset, so a real, field-verifiable location is always available by construction. A fixed radius of <strong>150 metres</strong> around that recorded point was defined as the boundary of "the site" for field purposes. This distance was set after checking how closely spaced known IDP and returnee sites are to one another nationally, so that it is wide enough to comfortably cover a typical site's extent while remaining unlikely to spill into a neighbouring, distinct site recorded separately in the same area. In a minority of cases, a single selected grid cell corresponded to more than one recorded site nearby; in those cases, the larger site by reported household count was used as the cluster's location, with the presence of the smaller, nearby site retained as supplementary information rather than discarded.</p>
<p>*[Map: Example IDP cluster — hexagon boundary, the site's GPS point, and the 150m field radius. See output/methodology_map_idp_site.png.]*</p>
<p>*[Map: Close-up of the 150m site radius around the IDP site GPS point. See output/methodology_map_idp_site_closeup.png.]*</p>
<p>Under this method, field teams are given the site's GPS point, the 150-metre radius, and a target number of interviews — six primary plus up to six reserve, matching the host protocol — and are responsible for constructing a short household list covering that site on arrival, then using a random selection procedure to choose which households on that list are interviewed. This is standard practice for camp- or site-based population assessments, where the population actually present at a location can shift over time, making a list built at the point of data collection more reliable than one derived in advance from remote imagery.</p>
<p>Both approaches produce the same underlying record structure — one row per planned interview, primary or reserve, each carrying a design weight and full administrative attribution down to ward level — so that host and IDP records can be analysed together as a single dataset despite having been generated through two different field procedures.</p>
<hr>
<h2>4. Known limitations and edge cases</h2>
<ul>
<li><p><strong>Certainty strata.</strong> A small number of strata — those with the smallest total household population — were sampled with certainty (every eligible location included) rather than through probability selection, because the underlying population was too small to draw a meaningful sample from. This is standard cluster-survey practice, but means the achieved sample size in those strata is fixed by the population itself rather than by the general sample size calculation.</p>
</li>
<li><p><strong>Repeated cluster selection.</strong> In strata where population is concentrated in a small number of very large grid cells, the same cell can be selected more than once by the systematic PPS procedure. Where this occurred, it was treated as a single cluster with a proportionally larger household quota rather than two separate field visits — the methodologically correct way to preserve each unit's true probability of selection under this design.</p>
</li>
<li><p><strong>Zero-eligible-location clusters (host).</strong> A small number of host clusters had no eligible building footprint at all and were reallocated to a different location within the same stratum, using the same selection method as the original draw (see Section 3). This does not affect the overall stratum sample size or the statistical properties of the design; it only affects which specific location represents that cluster's "slot" in the sample.</p>
</li>
<li><p><strong>Below-target clusters.</strong> A cluster is flagged as <strong>below-target</strong> (<code>below_target_cluster</code> in the dataset) when fewer eligible households were available in it than the six-household target: for host, fewer eligible buildings than needed within the hexagon; for IDP, a DTM-reported household count at the site below target. This is a cluster-level flag, not a comment on field staffing — it simply means that cluster's contribution to the sample was capped at whatever was genuinely available rather than a full six. In a small number of clusters — 85 out of 5,780, across both host and IDP — this occurred. These clusters take everything available rather than being padded artificially, and are flagged in the dataset so they can be reviewed, or supplemented in the field, as needed.</p>
</li>
<li><p><strong>Host strata in conflict-affected North-East LGAs: identified shortfall, corrected.</strong> For every host stratum, the calculated target sample size (Section 2) is at least 96 households — the threshold this assessment's validation process treats as a general minimum for stratum-level representativeness. In 10 of the 323 host strata, enough of that stratum's clusters were individually below-target that the <em>delivered</em> sample fell short of what a uniform six-households-per-cluster design would produce, despite the target itself being set correctly. Nine of the ten are in North-East LGAs with well-documented conflict-driven displacement and settlement destruction (eight in Borno, one in Yobe); the tenth is in Kano. The most plausible explanation is that satellite-derived building footprints undercount real households in these specific areas because structures have been destroyed, abandoned, or not rebuilt since displacement — the same underlying data-quality limitation already noted for IDP sites above, here affecting a small number of host locations within active or recently active conflict zones.</p>
<p>Recalculating the realized margin of error for each of these 10 LGAs — using the same formula, design effect, and intra-cluster correlation assumption as the original sample-size calculation, but evaluated at the sample actually achieved rather than the sample targeted — found that 6 of the 10 (Dikwa, Kala/Balge, Mafa, Marte, Ngala, and Nganzai) genuinely exceeded the study's 10% margin-of-error threshold; the other 4 (Abadam, Kaga, Makoda, and Bursari) were within tolerance (9.67–9.92%) even before any adjustment. Rather than leave six LGAs outside the target precision, an adjustment was applied uniformly across all 10: the primary household target per cluster was raised from six to seven for these strata specifically, and — for LGAs where even that increase left a residual shortfall — additional clusters were drawn from the same stratum using the identical population-proportional method as every other cluster in the design (see Section 2), adding real households rather than inflating existing clusters further. Cluster selection probabilities were recalculated for every hexagon in any stratum that received an added cluster, since adding a cluster changes that stratum's effective number of Stage-1 draws; this keeps design weights consistent with the actual number of clusters visited. A cluster size of seven was chosen over eight after comparing both directly: eight closed the gap with a little more room to spare, for a meaningfully larger increase in field burden, so seven was preferred as the smaller sufficient adjustment — 421 additional interviews above the original 17-cluster, six-per-cluster baseline across these 10 strata combined.</p>
<table>
<tr><th>State</th><th>LGA</th><th>ICC</th><th>DEFF (before, m=6)</th><th>Sample before</th><th>MoE before</th><th>DEFF (after, m=7)</th><th>Sample after</th><th>MoE after</th></tr>
<tr><td>Borno</td><td>Abadam</td><td>0.06</td><td>1.30</td><td>93</td><td>9.67%</td><td>1.36</td><td>119</td><td>8.73%</td></tr>
<tr><td>Borno</td><td>Dikwa</td><td>0.06</td><td>1.30</td><td>76</td><td><strong>10.74%</strong></td><td>1.36</td><td>123</td><td>8.62%</td></tr>
<tr><td>Borno</td><td>Kaga</td><td>0.06</td><td>1.30</td><td>89</td><td>9.92%</td><td>1.36</td><td>124</td><td>8.59%</td></tr>
<tr><td>Borno</td><td>Kala/Balge</td><td>0.06</td><td>1.30</td><td>59</td><td><strong>12.16%</strong></td><td>1.36</td><td>119</td><td>8.72%</td></tr>
<tr><td>Borno</td><td>Mafa</td><td>0.06</td><td>1.30</td><td>76</td><td><strong>10.72%</strong></td><td>1.36</td><td>122</td><td>8.64%</td></tr>
<tr><td>Borno</td><td>Marte</td><td>0.06</td><td>1.30</td><td>81</td><td><strong>10.40%</strong></td><td>1.36</td><td>122</td><td>8.66%</td></tr>
<tr><td>Borno</td><td>Ngala</td><td>0.06</td><td>1.30</td><td>56</td><td><strong>12.49%</strong></td><td>1.36</td><td>125</td><td>8.51%</td></tr>
<tr><td>Borno</td><td>Nganzai</td><td>0.06</td><td>1.30</td><td>86</td><td><strong>10.10%</strong></td><td>1.36</td><td>121</td><td>8.70%</td></tr>
<tr><td>Kano</td><td>Makoda</td><td>0.06</td><td>1.30</td><td>91</td><td>9.82%</td><td>1.36</td><td>122</td><td>8.67%</td></tr>
<tr><td>Yobe</td><td>Bursari</td><td>0.06</td><td>1.30</td><td>93</td><td>9.71%</td><td>1.36</td><td>124</td><td>8.60%</td></tr>
</table>
<p>All 10 LGAs are now within the 10% margin-of-error target, converging to a tight, comfortable range (8.51–8.73%) regardless of how far short of target each one started. Each of the 10 needed at least one supplementary cluster beyond the seven-per-cluster increase alone to close its residual shortfall (Ngala needed six), which is why the achieved sample tracks each LGA's true target rather than the uniform six-per-cluster baseline. The intra-cluster correlation coefficient (ICC = 0.06) is unchanged — only the cluster size (and, in the design effect column, its consequence) and the achieved sample itself changed; this is a like-for-like adjustment within the existing design, not a change to any underlying assumption. The margin-of-error figures shown incorporate the finite-population correction from each LGA's true household population, consistent with the original sample-size formula.</p>
<p>For context, a separate and much larger group — 124 of the assessment's 242 IDP strata — also deliver fewer than 96 households, but for an entirely different reason that does not indicate a shortfall: the calculated target itself is below 96 in those strata, because the DTM-recorded IDP population within that specific LGA is genuinely small. Unlike the ten host strata above, nothing was "lost" between target and delivery in these IDP strata — the design intentionally scales the sample to the population actually recorded there rather than imposing a fixed minimum that would oversample small IDP populations relative to their true size.</p>
</li>
<li><p><strong>Border-buffer and inaccessible-area exclusions.</strong> Two separate rules define the accessible sampling universe, both developed in consultation with FACT Foundation, the assessment's operational partner responsible for field team coordination. First, a buffer zone was excluded along Nigeria's international borders with all four neighbouring countries: 20km along the border with Niger, and 5km along the borders with Chad, Cameroon and Benin. Second, a set of Admin-3 areas within the North-East region, separately assessed by FACT as either inaccessible or not currently existing as functioning settlements, were excluded entirely. This second, assessment-based exclusion is currently available for the North-East only — in North-Central and North-West, the border buffer is the only exclusion applied, and no equivalent field-verified accessibility assessment has yet been incorporated for those two regions.</p>
</li>
<li><p><strong>Administrative boundary coverage.</strong> The nationally standard ward-level (Admin-3) administrative boundary dataset is only officially available for the North-East region. A secondary, nationally complete ward boundary source was used as the primary basis for ward-level attribution across all three regions, with the official dataset retained as a secondary cross-reference in the North-East specifically.</p>
</li>
<li><p><strong>Source-tile boundary handling (host).</strong> The satellite-derived building footprint dataset is delivered as several large geographic tiles that overlap slightly at their edges, and each tile is itself processed in smaller pieces. A cluster whose eligible buildings happened to fall across more than one of these pieces was, during quality assurance, found in a subset of cases to have its eligible-building pool and Stage 2 selection probability calculated from an incomplete portion of its true buildings. This was identified and corrected prior to finalising the sample: every cluster's eligible-building pool is now consolidated across all relevant source pieces before households are drawn and weights are calculated, and the corrected frame was independently re-verified, building by building, before delivery.</p>
</li>
<li><p><strong>Provisional IDP household counts.</strong> The number of households used to calculate the within-cluster selection probability for IDP clusters is currently the figure reported in the DTM dataset at the time the sample was drawn, not a count confirmed by field teams on the ground. This is addressed directly in the weighting section below.</p>
</li>
</ul>
<hr>
<h2>5. Weighting approach</h2>
<p>The design weight for each planned interview reflects both stages of selection, and is calculated as the inverse of the overall probability that the household would be selected: one divided by the probability of its cluster being selected, multiplied by the probability of the household being selected within that cluster.</p>
<p>The <strong>cluster-level (Stage 1) selection probability</strong> is 1 for every cluster in a certainty stratum, since every eligible location in that stratum was included with certainty. In PPS strata, it is proportional to the cluster's estimated household population relative to the total estimated household population of the stratum, scaled by the number of clusters drawn from that stratum — consistent with the population-proportional selection method described in Section 2.</p>
<p>The <strong>household-level (Stage 2) selection probability</strong> is the ratio of the number of households targeted for interview in that cluster (six, or seven in the 10 adjusted strata, or fewer for a below-target cluster) to the total number of eligible households identified within it — the count of eligible building footprints for host clusters, or the DTM-reported household count for IDP clusters.</p>
<p>The resulting design weight should be applied in any survey-weighted analysis of the collected data, together with the stratum (Admin-2 by population type) and cluster identifiers, so that estimates and their confidence intervals correctly account for the clustered, stratified, unequal-probability nature of the design, rather than treating the collected interviews as a simple random sample.</p>
<p>Because both selection probabilities can vary substantially across clusters — population density varies widely between a dense urban grid cell and a sparse rural one, and a fixed six-household target is drawn against that varying pool — the resulting design weights span a wide range, from 1 (a certainty-stratum or fully-enumerated small cluster) up to several thousand for the largest, most densely built clusters in PPS strata. This is an expected consequence of the design rather than an error, but it does imply a non-trivial design effect: analysts should expect the effective sample size for weighted indicator estimates to be smaller than the nominal number of completed interviews, and should check the realised design effect once data collection is complete.</p>
<p><strong>One point requires attention before weights are finalised for analysis.</strong> For IDP clusters, the household count used in the Stage 2 selection probability is currently the DTM's own reported estimate for that site, not a count confirmed on the ground. Once field teams report the actual number of households present at each IDP site at the time of listing, the Stage 2 selection probability — and therefore the final design weight — for IDP interviews should be recalculated using that confirmed figure in place of the DTM estimate, before any weighted analysis of IDP data is treated as final.</p>
<hr>
<h2>6. Detailed sample allocation by stratum</h2>
<p>The three tables below give the full stratum-level detail behind the design described in Sections 1–5, for validation and cross-reference. Every Admin-2 (LGA) in the assessment area appears once, with host and IDP figures shown side by side where both populations were sampled there — a dash indicates that population type was not present or not sampled in that LGA. Rows are grouped by region, then by state, with subtotals at each level; Table 3 rolls the same figures up to region level only. The underlying row-level data — including the design effect, intra-cluster correlation, and both the originally-calculated and finally-achieved cluster counts and sample sizes per stratum — is also provided as a standalone CSV (<code>strata_level_sampling_frame.csv</code>) alongside this document, intended for direct use by a validation team.</p>
